<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Kilogram</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Kilograms&amp;redirect=no" title="Kilograms">Kilograms</a>)</span></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>

</table>
<p>The <b>kilogram</b> or <b>kilogramme</b> (SI <a href="/wiki/Units_of_measurement" title="Units of measurement">unit</a> symbol: <b>kg</b>) is the <a href="/wiki/SI_base_unit" title="SI base unit">base unit</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> in the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a> (SI), and is defined as being equal to the mass of the <i>International Prototype of the Kilogram</i> (<b>IPK</b>, also known as "Le Grand K" or "Big K").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup> stored by the <a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a> at <a href="/wiki/Saint-Cloud" title="Saint-Cloud">Saint-Cloud</a>, France. It is the only metric base unit which is defined by an artifact.</p>
<p>It is expected that the <a href="/wiki/Proposed_redefinition_of_SI_base_units" title="Proposed redefinition of SI base units">definition of the kilogram and several other units will change</a> on May 20, 2019, following a final vote by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CGPM" title="CGPM">CGPM</a> in November 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> The new definition will use only invariant quantities of nature: the <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">Planck constant</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Caesium_standard" title="Caesium standard">caesium hyperfine frequency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brochure9_2016_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brochure9_2016-5">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Avoirdupois" title="Avoirdupois">avoirdupois</a> (or <i>international</i><span style="margin-left:0.1em">)</span> <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">pound</a>, used in both the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_units" title="Imperial units">imperial</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_customary_units" title="United States customary units">US customary</a> systems, is defined as exactly <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999453592370000000♠</span>0.453<span style="margin-left:.25em;">592</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">37</span> kg</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> making one kilogram approximately equal to 2.2046 avoirdupois pounds. Other traditional units of weight and mass around the world are also defined in terms of the kilogram, making the IPK the <a href="/wiki/Primary_standard" title="Primary standard">primary standard</a> for virtually all units of mass on Earth.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">gram</a>, 1/1000 of a kilogram, was provisionally defined in 1795 as the mass of one cubic <a href="/wiki/Centimetre" title="Centimetre">centimetre</a> of water at the melting point of ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> The final kilogram, manufactured as a prototype in 1799 and from which the IPK was derived in 1875, had a mass equal to the mass of 1 dm<sup>3</sup> of water at its maximum density, approximately 4 <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">°C</a>.</p>
<p>The kilogram is the only SI base unit with an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI_prefix" title="SI prefix">SI prefix</a> ("kilo", symbol "k") as part of its name. It is also the only SI unit that is still directly defined by an artifact rather than a fundamental physical property that can be reproduced in different laboratories. Three other base units (<a href="/wiki/Candela" title="Candela">cd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">A</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mol</a>) and 17 derived units (<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">N</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">Pa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">J</a>, <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">W</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coulomb" title="Coulomb">C</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">V</a>, <a href="/wiki/Farad" title="Farad">F</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohm" title="Ohm">Ω</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siemens_(unit)" title="Siemens (unit)">S</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weber_(unit)" title="Weber (unit)">Wb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">T</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_(unit)" title="Henry (unit)">H</a>, <a href="/wiki/Katal" title="Katal">kat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gray_(unit)" title="Gray (unit)">Gy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sievert" title="Sievert">Sv</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lumen_(unit)" title="Lumen (unit)">lm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lux" title="Lux">lx</a>) in the SI system are defined relative to the kilogram, so its stability is important. Only 8 other units do not require the kilogram in their definition: temperature (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kelvin_(unit)" title="Kelvin (unit)">K</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">°C</a>), time and frequency (<a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">s</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hertz_(unit)" title="Hertz (unit)">Hz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">Bq</a>), length (<a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">m</a>), and angle (<a href="/wiki/Radian" title="Radian">rad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steradian" title="Steradian">sr</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIbrochure8th_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-SIbrochure8th-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:112–118</sup></p>
<p>The International Prototype Kilogram was commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">General Conference on Weights and Measures</a> (CGPM) under the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Metre_Convention" title="Metre Convention">Metre Convention</a> (1875), and in the custody of the <a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a> (BIPM) who hold it on behalf of the CGPM. After the International Prototype Kilogram had been found to vary in mass over time relative to its reproductions, the <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_for_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Committee for Weights and Measures">International Committee for Weights and Measures</a> (CIPM) recommended in 2005 that the kilogram be redefined in terms of a fundamental constant of nature. At its 2011 meeting, the CGPM agreed in principle that the kilogram should be redefined in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">Planck constant</a>. The decision was originally deferred until 2014; in 2014 it was deferred again until the next meeting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> There are currently several different proposals for the redefinition (some of which have been abandoned); these are described in the <a href="/wiki/Kilogram#Proposed_future_definitions" title="Kilogram">Proposed Future Definitions</a> section below.</p>
<p>The International Prototype Kilogram (IPK) is rarely used or handled. Copies of the IPK kept by <a href="/wiki/Metrology" title="Metrology">national metrology laboratories</a> around the world were compared with the IPK in 1889, 1948, and 1989 to provide <a href="/wiki/Traceability" title="Traceability">traceability</a> of measurements of mass anywhere in the world back to the IPK.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_terminology">Name and terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Name and terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word <i>kilogramme</i> or <i>kilogram</i> is derived from the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <i>kilogramme</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-10">[9]</a></sup> which itself was a learned coinage, prefixing the <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Greek</a> stem of <span lang="grc">χίλιοι <i>khilioi</i></span> "a thousand" to <i>gramma</i>, a Late Latin term for "a small weight", itself from Greek <span lang="grc">γράμμα</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> The word <i>kilogramme</i> was written into French law in 1795, in the <i>Decree of <a href="/wiki/French_Republican_Calendar" title="French Republican Calendar">18 Germinal</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> which revised the older system of units introduced by the French <a href="/wiki/National_Convention" title="National Convention">National Convention</a> in 1793, where the <i>gravet</i> had been defined as weight (<i>poids</i>) of a cubic centimetre of water, equal to 1/1000 of a <i><a href="/wiki/Grave_(unit)" title="Grave (unit)">grave</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> In the decree of 1795, the term <i>gramme</i> thus replaced <i>gravet</i>, and <i>kilogramme</i> replaced <i>grave</i>.</p>
<p>The French spelling was adopted in the United Kingdom when the word was used for the first time in English in 1797,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-10">[9]</a></sup> with the spelling <i>kilogram</i> being adopted in the United States. In the United Kingdom both spellings are used, with "kilogram" having become by far the more common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[Note 2]</a></sup> UK law regulating the units to be used when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weights_and_Measures_Acts_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Weights and Measures Acts of the United Kingdom">trading by weight or measure</a> does not prevent the use of either spelling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 19th century the French word <i>kilo</i>, a <a href="/wiki/Clipping_(morphology)" title="Clipping (morphology)">shortening</a> of <i>kilogramme</i>, was imported into the English language where it has been used to mean both kilogram<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup> and kilometre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup> While <i>kilo</i> is acceptable in many generalist texts, for example <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup> its use is typically considered inappropriate in certain applications including scientific, technical and legal writing, where authors should adhere strictly to SI nomenclature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> gave the metric system legal status in 1866, it permitted the use of the word <i>kilo</i> as an alternative to the word <i>kilogram</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> but in 1990 revoked the status of the word <i>kilo</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 19th century, the standard system of metric units was the <a href="/wiki/Centimetre%E2%80%93gram%E2%80%93second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre–gram–second system of units">centimetre–gram–second system of units</a>, treating the <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">gram</a> as the fundamental unit of mass and the <i>kilogram</i> simply as a derived unit. In 1901, however, following the discoveries by <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> to the effect that electric measurements could not be explained in terms of the three fundamental units of length, mass and time, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Giorgi" title="Giovanni Giorgi">Giovanni Giorgi</a> proposed a new standard system that would include a fourth fundamental unit to measure quantities in electromagnetism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> In 1935 this was adopted by the <a href="/wiki/International_Electrotechnical_Commission" title="International Electrotechnical Commission">IEC</a> as the <i>Giorgi system</i>, now also known as <a href="/wiki/MKS_system_of_units" title="MKS system of units">MKS system</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> and in 1946 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CIPM" title="CIPM">CIPM</a> approved a proposal to adopt the <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">ampere</a> as the electromagnetic unit of the "MKSA system".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIbrochure8th_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-SIbrochure8th-8">[7]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:109,110</sup> In 1948 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CGPM" title="CGPM">CGPM</a> commissioned the CIPM "to make recommendations for a single practical system of units of measurement, suitable for adoption by all countries adhering to the Metre Convention".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> This led to the launch of SI in 1960 and the subsequent publication of the "SI Brochure", which stated that "It is not permissible to use abbreviations for unit symbols or unit names ...".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[Note 3]</a></sup> The CGS and MKS systems co-existed during much of the early-to-mid 20th century, but as a result of the decision to adopt the "Giorgi system" as the international system of units in 1960, the kilogram is now the <a href="/wiki/SI_base_unit" title="SI base unit">SI base unit</a> for mass, while the definition of the gram is derived from that of the kilogram.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_of_mass">Nature of mass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Nature of mass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:288px;max-width:288px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:142px;max-width:142px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kitchen_scale_20101110.jpg"><img alt="A kitchen scale with one scalepans and a dial to indicate the weight" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1500" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Kitchen_scale_20101110.jpg/140px-Kitchen_scale_20101110.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Kitchen_scale_20101110.jpg/210px-Kitchen_scale_20101110.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Kitchen_scale_20101110.jpg/280px-Kitchen_scale_20101110.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Measurement of <i>weight</i> – gravitational attraction of the measurand causes a distortion of the spring</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:142px;max-width:142px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HK_Museum_of_History_Steelyard_balance.JPG"><img alt="A beam balance with two scalepans and a selection of weights." data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/HK_Museum_of_History_Steelyard_balance.JPG/140px-HK_Museum_of_History_Steelyard_balance.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/HK_Museum_of_History_Steelyard_balance.JPG/210px-HK_Museum_of_History_Steelyard_balance.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/HK_Museum_of_History_Steelyard_balance.JPG/280px-HK_Museum_of_History_Steelyard_balance.JPG 2x" width="140"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Measurement of <i>mass</i> – the gravitational force on the measurand is balanced against the gravitational force on the weights.</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mass_versus_weight" title="Mass versus weight">Mass versus weight</a></div>
<p>The kilogram is a unit of <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>, a property corresponding to the common perception of how "heavy" an object is. Mass is an <i><a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertial</a></i> property; that is, it is related to the tendency of an object at rest to remain at rest, or if in motion to remain in motion at a constant velocity, unless acted upon by a <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a>. According to "<a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton's laws of motion">Newton's laws of motion</a>" and the equation <span class="nowrap"><i>F</i> = <i>ma</i></span>, (second law of motion) when acted upon by a force <i>F</i> of one <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">newton</a>, an object with mass <i>m</i> of one kilogram will <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">accelerate</a> <i>a</i> at the rate of one <a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second_squared" title="Metre per second squared">metre per second per second</a> (1 m/s<sup>2</sup>)—about one-tenth the acceleration due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_gravity" title="Earth's gravity">Earth's gravity</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[Note 4]</a></sup></p>
<p>While the <i><a href="/wiki/Weight" title="Weight">weight</a></i> of an object is dependent upon the strength of the local <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">gravitational field</a>, the <i>mass</i> of an object is independent of gravity, as mass is a measure of how much matter an object contains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[Note 5]</a></sup> Accordingly, for astronauts in <a href="/wiki/Micro-g_environment" title="Micro-g environment">microgravity</a>, no effort is required to hold objects off the cabin floor; they are "weightless". However, since objects in microgravity still retain their mass and inertia, an astronaut must exert ten times as much force to accelerate a 10‑kilogram object at the same rate as a 1‑kilogram object.</p>
<p>Because at any given point on Earth the weight of an object is proportional to its mass, the mass of an object in kilograms is usually measured by comparing its weight to the weight of a standard mass, whose mass is known in kilograms, using a device called a <a href="/wiki/Weighing_scale" title="Weighing scale">weighing scale</a>. The ratio of the force of gravity on the two objects, measured by the scale, is equal to the ratio of their masses.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kilogramme_des_Archives"><i>Kilogramme des Archives</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Kilogramme des Archives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For more on the history of the kilogram, see <a href="/wiki/Grave_(unit)" title="Grave (unit)">grave (unit)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arago_kilogram.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="178" data-file-width="450" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Arago_kilogram.jpg/290px-Arago_kilogram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Arago_kilogram.jpg/435px-Arago_kilogram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Arago_kilogram.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arago_kilogram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Arago kilogram, an exact copy of the "Kilogramme des Archives" commissioned in 1821 by the US under supervision of French physicist <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Arago" title="François Arago">François Arago</a> that served as the US's first kilogram standard of mass until 1889, when the US converted to primary metric standards and received its current kilogram prototypes, K4 and K20.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On April 7, 1795, the <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">gram</a> was decreed in France to be "the absolute weight of a volume of pure water equal to the cube of the hundredth part of the metre, and at the temperature of melting ice".<span style="margin-left:0.1em"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-decree_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-decree-31">[26]</a></sup></span> The concept of using a unit volume of water to define a unit measure of mass was proposed by the English philosopher <a href="/wiki/John_Wilkins" title="John Wilkins">John Wilkins</a> in his 1668 essay as a means of linking mass and length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilk1_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilk1-32">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilk2_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilk2-33">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since trade and commerce typically involve items significantly more massive than one gram, and since a mass standard made of water would be inconvenient and unstable, the regulation of commerce necessitated the manufacture of a <i>practical realization</i> of the water-based definition of mass. Accordingly, a provisional mass standard was made as a single-piece, metallic artifact one thousand times as massive as the gram—the kilogram.</p>
<p>At the same time, work was commissioned to precisely determine the mass of a cubic decimetre (one <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litre</a>) of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[Note 6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-decree_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-decree-31">[26]</a></sup> Although the decreed definition of the kilogram specified water at 0<span class="nowrap"> </span>°C—its highly stable <i>temperature</i> point—the French chemist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_Lef%C3%A8vre-Gineau" title="Louis Lefèvre-Gineau">Louis Lefèvre-Gineau</a> and the Italian naturalist <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Fabbroni" title="Giovanni Fabbroni">Giovanni Fabbroni</a> after several years of research chose to redefine the standard in 1799 to water's most stable <i>density</i> point: the temperature at which water reaches maximum density, which was measured at the time as 4<span class="nowrap"> </span>°C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[Note 7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[29]</a></sup> They concluded that one cubic decimetre of water at its maximum density was equal to 99.9265% of the target mass of the provisional kilogram standard made four years earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[Note 8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[30]</a></sup> That same year, 1799, an all-platinum kilogram prototype was fabricated with the objective that it would equal, as close as was scientifically feasible for the day, the mass of one cubic decimetre of water at 4<span class="nowrap"> </span>°C. The prototype was presented to the Archives of the Republic in June and on December 10, 1799, the prototype was formally ratified as the <i>kilogramme des Archives</i> (Kilogram of the Archives) and the kilogram was defined as being equal to its mass. This standard stood for the next 90 years.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_prototype_kilogram">International prototype kilogram</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: International prototype kilogram">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><span id="International_prototype_kilogram"></span></p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"IPK" redirects here. For the plastic material, see <a href="/wiki/IPK_Acrylic-polyvinyl_chloride" title="IPK Acrylic-polyvinyl chloride">IPK Acrylic-polyvinyl chloride</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CGKilogram.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1400" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/CGKilogram.jpg/220px-CGKilogram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/CGKilogram.jpg/330px-CGKilogram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/CGKilogram.jpg/440px-CGKilogram.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CGKilogram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Computer-generated_imagery" title="Computer-generated imagery">computer-generated image</a> of the international prototype kilogram (the inch ruler is for scale). The prototype is manufactured from a <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>–<a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a> alloy and is 39.17 mm in both diameter and height, its edges have a four-angle (22.5°, 45°, 67.5° and 79°) <a href="/wiki/Chamfer" title="Chamfer">chamfer</a> to minimize wear.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since 1889 the magnitude of the kilogram has been defined as the mass of an object called the <i>international prototype kilogram</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FirstCGPM_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FirstCGPM-39">[31]</a></sup> often referred to in the professional <a href="/wiki/Metrology" title="Metrology">metrology</a> world as the "IPK". The IPK is made of a platinum <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloy</a> known as "Pt‑10Ir", which is 90% <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> and 10% <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a> (by mass) and is machined into a right-circular cylinder (height = diameter) of about 39<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Millimetre" title="Millimetre">millimetres</a> to minimize its surface area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quinn_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quinn-40">[32]</a></sup> The addition of 10% iridium improved upon the all-platinum Kilogram of the Archives by greatly increasing <a href="/wiki/Hardness" title="Hardness">hardness</a> while still retaining platinum's many virtues: extreme resistance to <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidation</a>, extremely high <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> (almost twice as dense as <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> and more than 21 times as dense as <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water" title="Properties of water">water</a>), satisfactory <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivities</a>, and low <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_susceptibility" title="Magnetic susceptibility">magnetic susceptibility</a>. The IPK and its six sister copies are stored at the <a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a> (known by its French-language initials BIPM) in an environmentally monitored safe in the lower vault located in the basement of the BIPM's <a href="/wiki/Pavillon_de_Breteuil" title="Pavillon de Breteuil">Pavillon de Breteuil</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saint-Cloud" title="Saint-Cloud">Saint-Cloud</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[Note 9]</a></sup> on the outskirts of Paris (see <i><a href="#External_links">External images</a></i>, below, for photographs). Three independently controlled keys are required to open the vault. Official copies of the IPK were made available to other nations to serve as their national standards. These are compared to the IPK roughly every 40 years, thereby providing <a href="/wiki/Traceability" title="Traceability">traceability</a> of local measurements back to the IPK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[33]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prototype_kilogram_replica.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Prototype_kilogram_replica.JPG/220px-Prototype_kilogram_replica.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Prototype_kilogram_replica.JPG/330px-Prototype_kilogram_replica.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Prototype_kilogram_replica.JPG/440px-Prototype_kilogram_replica.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prototype_kilogram_replica.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A replica of the prototype kilogram on display at <a href="/wiki/Cit%C3%A9_des_Sciences_et_de_l%27Industrie" title="Cité des Sciences et de l'Industrie">Cité des Sciences et de l'Industrie</a>, featuring the protective double glass bell.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Metre_Convention" title="Metre Convention">Metre Convention</a> was signed on May 20, 1875 and further formalized the <a href="/wiki/Metric_system" title="Metric system">metric system</a> (a predecessor to the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">SI</a>), quickly leading to the production of the IPK. The IPK is one of three cylinders made in 1879 by <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Matthey" title="Johnson Matthey">Johnson Matthey</a>, which continues to manufacture nearly all of the national prototypes today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[35]</a></sup> In 1883, the mass of the IPK was found to be indistinguishable from that of the <i>Kilogramme des Archives</i> made eighty-four years prior, and was formally ratified as <i>the</i> kilogram by the 1st <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">CGPM</a> in 1889.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quinn_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Quinn-40">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Modern measurements of <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Standard_Mean_Ocean_Water" title="Vienna Standard Mean Ocean Water">Vienna Standard Mean Ocean Water</a>, which is pure distilled water with an <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopic</a> composition representative of the average of the world's oceans, show it has a density of <span class="nowrap"><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999999975000000000♠</span>0.999<span style="margin-left:.25em;">975</span></span> ±<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6994100000000000000♠</span>0.000<span style="margin-left:.25em;">001</span></span> kg/L</span> at its point of maximum density (3.984 °C) under one <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">standard atmosphere</a> (101 325 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">Pa</a> or 760 <a href="/wiki/Torr" title="Torr">torr</a>) of pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[36]</a></sup> Thus, a cubic decimetre of water at its point of maximum density is only 25 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">parts per million</a> less massive than the IPK; that is to say, the 25 milligram difference shows that the scientists over 218 years ago managed to make the mass of the Kilogram of the Archives equal that of a cubic decimetre of water at 4 °C, with a margin of error <i>at most</i> within the mass of a single excess grain of rice.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Copies_of_the_international_prototype_kilogram">Copies of the international prototype kilogram</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Copies of the international prototype kilogram">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:214px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:National_prototype_kilogram_K20_replica.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="212" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/85/National_prototype_kilogram_K20_replica.jpg" width="212"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:National_prototype_kilogram_K20_replica.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
National prototype kilogram K20, one of two prototypes stored at the US <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">National Institute of Standards and Technology</a> in Gaithersburg, Maryland, which serve as primary standards for defining all units of mass and weight in the United States. This is a replica for public display, shown as it is normally stored, under two bell jars.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The various copies of the international prototype kilogram are given the following designations in the literature:</p>
<ul>
<li>The IPK itself. Located in Saint-Cloud, France.</li>
<li>Six sister copies, numbered: K1, 7, 8(41),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-n841_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-n841-46">[Note 10]</a></sup> 32, 43 and 47.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[37]</a></sup> Located in Saint-Cloud, France.</li>
<li>Ten working copies, eight (9, 31, 42′, 63, 77, 88, 91, and 650<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-n650_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-n650-48">[Note 11]</a></sup>) for routine use and two (25 and 73) for special use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stock_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stock-49">[38]</a></sup> Located in Saint-Cloud, France.</li>
<li>National prototypes, stored in<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Girard_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Girard-50">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> (44 and 87), <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> (49), <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> (28 and 37), <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> (66), <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> (50 and 74), <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (60 and 64; 75 in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>), <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> (67), <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> (48), <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> (58), <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> (23), <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> (35), <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> (52, 55 and 70), <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> (16), <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> (57), <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> (46), <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> (71), <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> (5 and 76), <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> (6 and 94), <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> (95), <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> (21, 90 and 96), <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> (53), <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a> (68), <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> (36), <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> (93), <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> (51), <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> (69), <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> (2), <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> (12 and 26<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[43]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> (11 and 29), <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> (83), <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> (41 and 65), <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> (56), <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> (39, 72 and 84), <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> (24 and 3), <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> (40 and 86),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> (38 and 89), <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> (78), <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> (80), <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> (54<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[45]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> (18,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[46]</a></sup> 81 and 82) and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (20,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jabbour_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jabbour-58">[47]</a></sup> 4, 79, 85 and 92).</li>
<li>Some additional copies held by non-national organizations, such as the <a href="/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences" title="French Academy of Sciences">French Academy of Sciences</a> in Paris (34) and the Istituto di Metrologia G. Colonnetti in Turin (62).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Girard_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Girard-50">[39]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stability_of_the_international_prototype_kilogram">Stability of the international prototype kilogram</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Stability of the international prototype kilogram">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By definition, the error in the measured value of the IPK's mass is exactly zero; the IPK <i>is</i> the kilogram. However, any changes in the IPK's mass over time can be deduced by comparing its mass to that of its official copies stored throughout the world, a rarely undertaken process called "periodic verification". The only three verifications occurred in 1889, 1948, and 1989. For instance, the US owns four <span class="nowrap">90% platinum /</span> 10%<span class="nowrap"> </span>iridium (Pt‑10Ir) kilogram standards, two of which, K4 and K20, are from the original batch of 40 replicas delivered in 1884.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[Note 12]</a></sup> The K20 prototype was designated as the primary national standard of mass for the US. Both of these, as well as those from other nations, are periodically returned to the BIPM for verification. Extraordinary care is exercised when transporting prototypes. In 1984, the K4 and K20 prototypes were hand-carried in the passenger section of separate commercial airliners.</p>
<p>Note that none of the replicas has a mass precisely equal to that of the IPK; their masses are calibrated and documented as offset values. For instance, K20, the US's primary standard, originally had an official mass of <span class="nowrap">1 kg − 39</span> micrograms (μg) in 1889; that is to say, K20 was 39<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg less than the IPK. A verification performed in 1948 showed a mass of <span class="nowrap">1 kg − 19 µg</span>. The latest verification performed in 1989 shows a mass precisely identical to its original 1889 value. Quite unlike transient variations such as this, the US's check standard, K4, has persistently declined in mass relative to the IPK—and for an identifiable reason. Check standards are used much more often than primary standards and are prone to scratches and other wear. K4 was originally delivered with an official mass of <span class="nowrap">1 kg − 75 µg</span> in 1889, but as of 1989 was officially calibrated at <span class="nowrap">1 kg − 106 µg</span> and ten years later was <span class="nowrap">1 kg − 116 µg.</span> Over a period of 110 years, K4 lost 41<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg relative to the IPK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Recalibration_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Recalibration-60">[48]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:401px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prototype_mass_drifts.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="790" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Prototype_mass_drifts.jpg/399px-Prototype_mass_drifts.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Prototype_mass_drifts.jpg/599px-Prototype_mass_drifts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c9/Prototype_mass_drifts.jpg 2x" width="399"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prototype_mass_drifts.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mass drift over time of national prototypes <span class="nowrap">K21–K40</span>, plus two of the IPK's sister copies: K32 and K8(41).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-n841_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-n841-46">[Note 10]</a></sup> All mass changes are relative to the IPK. The initial 1889 starting-value offsets relative to the IPK have been nulled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Girard_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-Girard-50">[39]</a></sup> The above are all <i>relative</i> measurements; no historical mass-measurement data is available to determine which of the prototypes has been most stable relative to an invariant of nature. There is the distinct possibility that <i>all</i> the prototypes gained mass over 100 years and that K21, K35, K40, and the IPK simply <i>gained less</i> than the others.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beyond the simple wear that check standards can experience, the mass of even the carefully stored national prototypes can drift relative to the IPK for a variety of reasons, some known and some unknown. Since the IPK and its replicas are stored in air (albeit under two or more nested <a href="/wiki/Bell_jar" title="Bell jar">bell jars</a>), they gain mass through <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">adsorption</a> of atmospheric contamination onto their surfaces. Accordingly, they are cleaned in a process the BIPM developed between 1939 and 1946 known as "the BIPM cleaning method"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[49]</a></sup> that comprises firmly rubbing with a <a href="/wiki/Chamois_leather" title="Chamois leather">chamois</a> soaked in equal parts <a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">ether</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Steam_cleaning" title="Steam cleaning">steam cleaning</a> with bi-<a href="/wiki/Distilled_water" title="Distilled water">distilled water</a>, and allowing the prototypes to settle for <span class="nowrap">7–10</span> days before verification. Before the BIPM's published report in 1994 detailing the relative change in mass of the prototypes, different standard bodies used different techniques to clean their prototypes. The NIST's practice before then was to soak and rinse its two prototypes first in <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a>, then in ethanol, and to then clean them with a jet of bi-distilled water steam. Cleaning the prototypes removes between 5 and 60<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg of contamination depending largely on the time elapsed since the last cleaning. Further, a second cleaning can remove up to 10<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg more. After cleaning—even when they are stored under their bell jars—the IPK and its replicas immediately begin gaining mass again. The BIPM even developed a model of this gain and concluded that it averaged 1.11<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg per month for the first 3 months after cleaning and then decreased to an average of about 1<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg per year thereafter. Since check standards like K4 are not cleaned for routine calibrations of other mass standards—a precaution to minimize the potential for wear and handling damage—the BIPM's model of time-dependent mass gain has been used as an "after cleaning" correction factor.</p>
<p>Because the first forty official copies are made of the same alloy as the IPK and are stored under similar conditions, periodic verifications using a large number of replicas—especially the national primary standards, which are rarely used—can convincingly demonstrate the stability of the IPK. What has become clear after the third periodic verification performed between 1988 and 1992 is that masses of the entire worldwide ensemble of prototypes have been slowly but inexorably diverging from each other. It is also clear that the mass of the IPK lost perhaps 50<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg over the last century, and possibly significantly more, in comparison to its official copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Girard_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-Girard-50">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Redef_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Redef-62">[50]</a></sup> The reason for this drift has eluded physicists who have dedicated their careers to the SI unit of mass. No plausible mechanism has been proposed to explain either a steady decrease in the mass of the IPK, or an increase in that of its replicas dispersed throughout the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[Note 13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis85_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis85-65">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conjecture_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-conjecture-66">[53]</a></sup> This <i>relative</i> nature of the changes amongst the world's kilogram prototypes is often misreported in the popular press, and even some notable scientific magazines, which often state that the IPK simply "lost 50<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg" and omit the very important caveat of "<i>in comparison to its official copies</i>".<span style="margin-left:0.2em">Even well respected organizations incorrectly represent the relative nature of the mass divergence as being one of mass loss, as exemplified by <a class="external text" href="http://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2007/09/070921110735.htm" rel="nofollow">this site at Science Daily</a>, and <a class="external text" href="http://www.physorg.com/news109595312.html" rel="nofollow">this site at PhysOrg.com</a>, and <a class="external text" href="http://www.sandia.gov/LabNews/080201.html" rel="nofollow">this site at Sandia National Laboratories.</a> The root of the problem is often the reporters' failure to correctly interpret or paraphrase nuanced scientific concepts, as exemplified by <a class="external text" href="http://www.physorg.com/news108836759.html" rel="nofollow">this 12<span class="nowrap"> </span>September 2007 story</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> published on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PhysOrg.com" title="PhysOrg.com">PhysOrg.com</a>. In that AP story, Richard Davis—who used to be the NIST's kilogram specialist and now works for the BIPM in France—was correctly quoted by the AP when he stated that the mass change is a relative issue. Then the AP summarized the nature of issue with this lead-in to the story: "<i>A kilogram just isn't what it used to be. The 118-year-old cylinder that is the international prototype for the metric mass, kept tightly under lock and key outside Paris, is mysteriously losing weight — if ever so slightly</i>". Like many of the above-linked sites, the AP also misreported the age of the IPK, using the date of its adoption as the mass prototype, not the date of the cylinder's manufacture. This is a mistake even <a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a> fell victim to in a print edition.</span> Moreover, there are no technical means available to determine whether or not the entire worldwide ensemble of prototypes suffers from even greater long-term trends upwards or downwards because their mass "relative to an invariant of nature is unknown at a level below 1000<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg over a period of 100 or even 50 years".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Redef_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Redef-62">[50]</a></sup> Given the lack of data identifying which of the world's kilogram prototypes has been most stable in absolute terms, it is equally valid to state that the first batch of replicas has, as a group, gained an average of about 25<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg over one hundred years in comparison to the IPK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[Note 14]</a></sup></p>
<p>What <i>is</i> known specifically about the IPK is that it exhibits a short-term instability of about 30<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg over a period of about a month in its after-cleaned mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[54]</a></sup> The precise reason for this short-term instability is not understood but is thought to entail surface effects: microscopic differences between the prototypes' polished surfaces, possibly aggravated by <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> absorption due to <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysis</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">volatile organic compounds</a> that slowly deposit onto the prototypes as well as the <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a>-based solvents used to clean them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conjecture_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-conjecture-66">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been possible to rule out many explanations of the observed divergences in the masses of the world's prototypes proposed by scientists and the general public. The BIPM's FAQ explains, for example, that the divergence is dependent on the amount of time elapsed between measurements and not dependent on the number of times the artifacts have been cleaned or possible changes in gravity or environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[56]</a></sup> Reports published in 2013 by Peter Cumpson of <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_University" title="Newcastle University">Newcastle University</a> based on the <a href="/wiki/X-ray_photoelectron_spectroscopy" title="X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy</a> of samples that were stored alongside various prototype kilograms suggested that one source of the divergence between the various prototypes could be traced to <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> that had been absorbed by the prototypes being in the proximity of mercury-based instruments. The IPK has been stored within centimetres of a mercury thermometer since at least as far back as the late 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[57]</a></sup> In this Newcastle University work six platinum weights made in the nineteenth century were all found to have mercury at the surface, the most contaminated of which had the equivalent of 250<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg of mercury when scaled to the surface area of a kilogram prototype.</p>
<p>Scientists are seeing far greater variability in the prototypes than previously believed. The increasing divergence in the masses of the world's prototypes and the short-term instability in the IPK has prompted research into improved methods to obtain a smooth surface finish using <a href="/wiki/Diamond_turning" title="Diamond turning">diamond turning</a> on newly manufactured replicas and has intensified the search for a new definition of the kilogram. See <i><a href="#Proposed_future_definitions">Proposed future definitions</a></i>, below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dependency_of_the_SI_on_the_IPK">Dependency of the SI on the IPK</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Dependency of the SI on the IPK">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:266px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SI_base_unit.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/SI_base_unit.svg/264px-SI_base_unit.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/SI_base_unit.svg/396px-SI_base_unit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/SI_base_unit.svg/528px-SI_base_unit.svg.png 2x" width="264"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SI_base_unit.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The magnitude of many of the units comprising the SI system of measurement, including most of those used in the measurement of electricity and light, are highly dependent upon the stability of a 138-year-old, golf-ball-sized cylinder of metal stored in a vault in France.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The stability of the IPK is crucial because the kilogram underpins much of the SI system of measurement as it is currently defined and structured. For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">newton</a> is defined as the force necessary to accelerate one kilogram at one <a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second_squared" title="Metre per second squared">metre per second squared</a>. If the mass of the IPK were to change slightly, so too must the newton by a proportional degree. In turn, the <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">pascal</a>, the SI unit of <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>, is defined in terms of the newton. This chain of dependency follows to many other SI units of measure. For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joule</a>, the SI unit of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>, is defined as that expended when a force of one newton acts through one <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">metre</a>. Next to be affected is the SI unit of <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">power</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watt</a>, which is one joule per second. The <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">ampere</a> too is defined relative to the newton, and ultimately, the kilogram.</p>
<p>With the magnitude of the primary units of electricity thus determined by the kilogram, so too follow many others, namely the <a href="/wiki/Coulomb" title="Coulomb">coulomb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">volt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">tesla</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Weber_(unit)" title="Weber (unit)">weber</a>. Even units used in the measure of light would be affected; the <a href="/wiki/Candela" title="Candela">candela</a>—following the change in the <span class="nowrap">watt—</span>would in turn affect the <a href="/wiki/Lumen_(unit)" title="Lumen (unit)">lumen</a> and<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Lux" title="Lux">lux</a>.</p>
<p>Because the magnitude of many of the units comprising the SI system of measurement is ultimately defined by the mass of a 138-year-old, golf-ball-sized piece of metal, the quality of the IPK must be diligently protected to preserve the integrity of the SI system. Yet, despite the best stewardship, the average mass of the worldwide ensemble of prototypes and the mass of the IPK have likely diverged another 6.7<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg since the third periodic verification 28<span class="nowrap"> </span>years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[Note 15]</a></sup> Further, the world's national metrology laboratories must wait for the fourth periodic verification to confirm whether the historical trends<sub><span class="nowrap"> </span></sub>persisted.</p>
<p>Fortunately, <i>definitions</i> of the SI units are quite different from their <i>practical realizations</i>. For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">metre</a> is <i>defined</i> as the distance light travels in a vacuum during a time interval of <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>299,792,458</sub></span> of a second. However, the metre's <i>practical realization</i> typically takes the form of a helium–neon laser, and the metre's length is <i>delineated</i>—not defined—as <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006157980029872800♠</span>1<span style="margin-left:.25em;">579</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">800</span>.298<span style="margin-left:.25em;">728</span></span> wavelengths of light from this laser. Now suppose that the official measurement of the second was found to have drifted by a few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" title="Parts per billion">parts per billion</a> (it is actually extremely stable with a reproducibility of a few parts in 10<sup>15</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[59]</a></sup> There would be no automatic effect on the metre because the second—and thus the metre's length—is abstracted via the laser comprising the metre's practical realization. Scientists performing metre calibrations would simply continue to measure out the same number of laser wavelengths until an agreement was reached to do otherwise. The same is true with regard to the real-world dependency on the kilogram: if the mass of the IPK was found to have changed slightly, there would be no automatic effect upon the other units of measure because their practical realizations provide an insulating layer of abstraction. Any discrepancy would eventually have to be reconciled though, because the virtue of the SI system is its precise mathematical and logical harmony amongst its units. If the IPK's value were definitively proven to have changed, one solution would be to simply redefine the kilogram as being equal to the mass of the IPK plus an offset value, similarly to what is currently done with its replicas; e.g., "the kilogram is equal to the mass of the <span class="nowrap">IPK + 42 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" title="Parts per billion">parts per billion</a>"</span> (equivalent to 42<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg).</p>
<p>The long-term solution to this problem, however, is to liberate the SI system's dependency on the IPK by developing a practical realization of the kilogram that can be reproduced in different laboratories by following a written specification. The units of measure in such a practical realization would have their magnitudes precisely defined and expressed in terms of <a href="/wiki/Dimensionless_physical_constant" title="Dimensionless physical constant">fundamental physical constants</a>. While major portions of the SI system would still be based on the kilogram, the kilogram would in turn be based on invariant, universal constants of nature. Much work towards that end is ongoing, though no alternative has yet achieved the uncertainty of 20 parts per billion (~20<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg) required to improve upon the IPK.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_future_definitions">Proposed future definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Proposed future definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Proposed_redefinition_of_SI_base_units" title="Proposed redefinition of SI base units">Proposed redefinition of SI base units</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CODATA_2018" title="CODATA 2018">CODATA 2018</a></div>
<dl>
<dd><i>In the following sections, wherever numeric equalities are shown in 'concise form'—such as <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7013185487000000000♠</span>1.854<span style="margin-left:.25em;">87</span>(14)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>13</sup></span>—the two digits between the parentheses denote the <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty" title="Uncertainty">uncertainty</a> at 1σ <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard deviation</a> (68% confidence level) in the two least significant digits of the <a href="/wiki/Significand" title="Significand">significand</a>. A final X in a proposed definition denotes digits yet to be agreed on.</i></dd>
</dl>
<p>As of 2014 the kilogram was the only SI unit still defined by an artifact. In 1960 the <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">metre</a> having previously also been defined by reference to an artifact (a single platinum-iridium bar with two marks on it) was redefined in terms of invariant, fundamental physical constants (the wavelength of a particular emission of light emitted by <a href="/wiki/Krypton" title="Krypton">krypton</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[60]</a></sup> and later the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>) so that the standard can be reproduced in different laboratories by following a written specification. At the 94th Meeting of the <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_for_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Committee for Weights and Measures">International Committee for Weights and Measures</a> (CIPM) (2005)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[61]</a></sup> it was recommended that the same be done with the kilogram.</p>
<p>In October 2010, the CIPM voted to submit a resolution for consideration at the <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">General Conference on Weights and Measures</a> (CGPM), to "take note of an intention" that the kilogram be defined in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">Planck constant</a>, <i>h</i> (which has dimensions of energy times time) together with other fundamental units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draft_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-draft-78">[63]</a></sup> This resolution was accepted by the 24th conference of the CGPM<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[64]</a></sup> in October 2011 and further discussed at the 25th conference in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[66]</a></sup> Although the Committee recognised that significant progress had been made, they concluded that the data did not appear sufficiently robust to adopt the revised definition, and that work should continue to enable the adoption at the 26th meeting, scheduled for 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80">[65]</a></sup> Such a definition would theoretically permit any apparatus that was capable of delineating the kilogram in terms of the Planck constant to be used as long as it possessed sufficient precision, accuracy and stability. The Kibble balance (discussed below) may be able to do this.</p>
<p>In the project to replace the last artifact that underpins much of the International System of Units (SI), a variety of other very different technologies and approaches were considered and explored over many years. They too are covered below. Some of these now-abandoned approaches were based on equipment and procedures that would have enabled the reproducible production of new, kilogram-mass prototypes on demand (albeit with extraordinary effort) using measurement techniques and material properties that are ultimately based on, or traceable to, fundamental constants. Others were based on devices that measured either the acceleration or weight of hand-tuned kilogram test masses and which expressed their magnitudes in electrical terms via special components that permit traceability to fundamental constants. All approaches depend on converting a weight measurement to a mass, and therefore require the precise measurement of the strength of gravity in laboratories. All approaches would have precisely fixed one or more constants of nature at a defined value.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kibble_balance">Kibble balance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Kibble balance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Watt_balance,_large_view.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="474" data-file-width="550" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Watt_balance%2C_large_view.jpg/310px-Watt_balance%2C_large_view.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Watt_balance%2C_large_view.jpg/465px-Watt_balance%2C_large_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/Watt_balance%2C_large_view.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Watt_balance,_large_view.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">NIST's</a> Kibble balance is a project of the US Government to develop an "electronic kilogram". The vacuum chamber dome, which lowers over the entire apparatus, is visible at top.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kibble_balance" title="Kibble balance">Kibble balance</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kibble_balance" title="Kibble balance">Kibble balance</a> (known as a "watt balance" before 2016) is essentially a <a href="/wiki/Mass_versus_weight#Types_of_scales_and_what_they_measure" title="Mass versus weight">single-pan</a> <a href="/wiki/Weighing_scale" title="Weighing scale">weighing scale</a> that measures the <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electric power</a> necessary to oppose the weight of a kilogram test mass as it is pulled by Earth's gravity. It is a variation of an <a href="/wiki/Ampere_balance" title="Ampere balance">ampere balance</a> in that it employs an extra calibration step that nulls the effect of geometry. The <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential" title="Electric potential">electric potential</a> in the Kibble balance is delineated by a <a href="/wiki/Josephson_effect" title="Josephson effect">Josephson voltage standard</a>, which allows voltage to be linked to an invariant constant of nature with extremely high precision and stability. Its circuit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">resistance</a> is calibrated against a <a href="/wiki/Hall_effect" title="Hall effect">quantum Hall</a> <a href="/wiki/Resistor#Resistance_standards" title="Resistor">resistance standard</a>.</p>
<p>The Kibble balance requires extremely precise measurement of the local gravitational acceleration <i><b>g</b></i> in the laboratory, using a <a href="/wiki/Gravimeter" title="Gravimeter">gravimeter</a>. For instance, the NIST compensates for Earth's gravity gradient of 309<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Gal_(unit)" title="Gal (unit)">µGal</a> per metre when the elevation of the center of the <a href="/wiki/Gravimeter" title="Gravimeter">gravimeter</a> differs from that of the nearby test mass in the Kibble balance; a change in the weight of a one-kilogram test mass that equates to about 316<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg/m.</p>
<p>In April 2007, the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">NIST's</a> implementation of the Kibble balance demonstrated a combined relative standard uncertainty (CRSU) of 36<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg and a short-term resolution of <span class="nowrap">10<sup>−15</sup></span><span class="nowrap"> </span>µg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEESteiner_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEEESteiner-82">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[Note 16]</a></sup> The UK's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Physical_Laboratory,_UK" title="National Physical Laboratory, UK">National Physical Laboratory's</a> Kibble balance demonstrated a CRSU of 70.3<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[68]</a></sup> That Kibble balance was disassembled and shipped in 2009 to Canada's Institute for National Measurement Standards (part of the <a href="/wiki/National_Research_Council_(Canada)" title="National Research Council (Canada)">National Research Council</a>), where research and development with the device could continue.</p>
<p>If the CGPM adopts the new proposal and the new definition of the kilogram becomes part of the SI, the value in SI units of the <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">Planck constant</a> (<i>h</i>), which is a measure that relates the energy of <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> to their frequency, would be precisely fixed (the currently accepted value of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6966662607004000000♠</span>6.626<span style="margin-left:.25em;">070</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">040</span>(81)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−34</sup> J⋅s</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physconst-h_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-physconst-h-85">[69]</a></sup> has an uncertainty of ± about 1 in 23 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[Note 17]</a></sup> Once agreed upon internationally, the kilogram would no longer be defined as the mass of the IPK. All the remaining units in the International System of Units (the SI) that today have dependencies upon the kilogram and the joule would also fall in place, their magnitudes ultimately defined, in part, in terms of photon oscillations rather than the IPK.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:241px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Michelson_Interferometer_Laser_Interference_Fringes-Red.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="484" data-file-width="597" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/90/Michelson_Interferometer_Laser_Interference_Fringes-Red.jpg/239px-Michelson_Interferometer_Laser_Interference_Fringes-Red.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/90/Michelson_Interferometer_Laser_Interference_Fringes-Red.jpg/359px-Michelson_Interferometer_Laser_Interference_Fringes-Red.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/90/Michelson_Interferometer_Laser_Interference_Fringes-Red.jpg/478px-Michelson_Interferometer_Laser_Interference_Fringes-Red.jpg 2x" width="239"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Michelson_Interferometer_Laser_Interference_Fringes-Red.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The local gravitational acceleration <i><b>g</b></i> is measured with exceptional precision with the help of a laser interferometer. The laser's pattern of <a href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference fringes</a>—the dark and light bands above—blooms at an ever-faster rate as a free-falling <a href="/wiki/Retroreflector" title="Retroreflector">corner reflector</a> drops inside an absolute <a href="/wiki/Gravimeter" title="Gravimeter">gravimeter</a>. The pattern's frequency sweep is timed by an atomic clock.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gravity and the nature of the Kibble balance, which oscillates test masses up and down against the local gravitational acceleration <i><b>g</b></i>, are exploited so that mechanical power is compared against electrical power, which is the square of voltage divided by electrical resistance. However, <i><b>g</b></i> varies significantly—by nearly 1%—depending on where on the Earth's surface the measurement is made (see <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_gravity" title="Earth's gravity">Earth's gravity</a></i>). There are also slight seasonal variations in <i><b>g</b></i> due to changes in underground water tables, and larger semimonthly and diurnal changes due to tidal distortions in the Earth's shape caused by the Moon. Although <i><b>g</b></i> would not be a term in the <i>definition</i> of the kilogram, it would be crucial in the <i>delineation</i><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> of the kilogram when relating energy to power. Accordingly, <i><b>g</b></i> must be measured with at least as much precision and accuracy as are the other terms, so measurements of <i><b>g</b></i> must also be traceable to fundamental constants of nature. For the most precise work in mass metrology, <i><b>g</b></i> is measured using dropping-mass absolute <a href="/wiki/Gravimeter" title="Gravimeter">gravimeters</a> that contain an iodine-stabilized <a href="/wiki/Helium%E2%80%93neon_laser" title="Helium–neon laser">helium–neon laser</a> <a href="/wiki/Interferometry" title="Interferometry">interferometer</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">fringe-signal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chirp" title="Chirp">frequency-sweep</a> output from the interferometer is measured with a rubidium <a href="/wiki/Atomic_clock" title="Atomic clock">atomic clock</a>. Since this type of dropping-mass gravimeter derives its accuracy and stability from the constancy of the speed of light as well as the innate properties of helium, neon, and rubidium atoms, the 'gravity' term in the delineation of an all-electronic kilogram is also measured in terms of invariants of nature—and with very high precision. For instance, in the basement of the NIST's Gaithersburg facility in 2009, when measuring the gravity acting upon Pt‑10Ir test masses (which are denser, smaller, and have a slightly lower center of gravity inside the Kibble balance than stainless steel masses), the measured value was typically within 8 ppb of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000980101644000000♠</span>9.801<span style="margin-left:.25em;">016</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">44</span> m/s<sup>2</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The virtue of electronic realizations like the Kibble balance is that the definition and dissemination of the kilogram would no longer be dependent upon the stability of kilogram prototypes, which must be very carefully handled and stored. It would free physicists from the need to rely on assumptions about the stability of those prototypes. Instead, hand-tuned, close-approximation mass standards would simply be weighed and documented as being equal to one kilogram plus an offset value. With the Kibble balance, while the kilogram would be <i>delineated</i> in electrical and gravity terms, all of which are traceable to invariants of nature; it would be <i>defined</i> in a manner that is directly traceable to just three fundamental constants of nature. The Planck constant defines the kilogram in terms of the second and the metre. By fixing the Planck constant, the <i>definition</i> of the kilogram would depend only on the <i>definitions</i> of the second and the metre. The definition of the second depends on a single defined physical constant: the ground state hyperfine splitting frequency of the caesium 133 atom Δ<i>ν</i>(<sup>133</sup>Cs)<sub>hfs</sub>. The metre depends on the second and on an additional defined physical constant: the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> <i>c</i>. If the kilogram is redefined in this manner, mass artifacts—physical objects calibrated in a Kibble balance, including the IPK—would no longer be part of the definition, but would instead become <i>transfer standards</i>.</p>
<p>Scales like the Kibble balance also permit more flexibility in choosing materials with especially desirable properties for mass standards. For instance, Pt‑10Ir could continue to be used so that the specific gravity of newly produced mass standards would be the same as existing national primary and check standards (≈21.55<span class="nowrap"> </span>g/ml). This would reduce the relative uncertainty when making <a href="/wiki/Mass_versus_weight#Buoyancy_and_weight" title="Mass versus weight">mass comparisons in air</a>. Alternatively, entirely different materials and constructions could be explored with the objective of producing mass standards with greater stability. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">osmium</a>-iridium alloys could be investigated if platinum's propensity to absorb hydrogen (due to catalysis of VOCs and hydrocarbon-based cleaning solvents) and atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> proved to be sources of instability. Also, vapor-deposited, protective ceramic coatings like <a href="/wiki/Titanium_nitride" title="Titanium nitride">nitrides</a> could be investigated for their suitability to isolate these new alloys.</p>
