<p>The challenge with Kibble balances is not only in reducing their uncertainty, but also in making them truly <i>practical</i> realizations of the kilogram. Nearly every aspect of Kibble balances and their support equipment requires such extraordinarily precise and accurate, state-of-the-art technology that—unlike a device like an atomic clock—few countries would currently choose to fund their operation. For instance, the NIST's Kibble balance used four resistance standards in 2007, each of which was rotated through the Kibble balance every two to six weeks after being calibrated in a different part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NIST#Organization" title="NIST">NIST headquarters</a> facility in <a href="/wiki/Gaithersburg,_Maryland" title="Gaithersburg, Maryland">Gaithersburg, Maryland</a>. It was found that simply moving the resistance standards down the hall to the Kibble balance after calibration altered their values 10<span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" title="Parts per billion">ppb</a> (equivalent to 10<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg) or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[71]</a></sup> Present-day technology is insufficient to permit stable operation of Kibble balances between even biannual calibrations. If the kilogram is defined in terms of the Planck constant, it is likely there will only be a few—at most—Kibble balances initially operating in the world.</p>
<p>Alternative approaches to redefining the kilogram that were fundamentally different from the Kibble balance were explored to varying degrees with some abandoned, as follows:</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atom-counting_approaches">Atom-counting approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Atom-counting approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon-12">Carbon-12</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Carbon-12">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Though not offering a practical realization, this definition would precisely define the magnitude of the kilogram in terms of a certain number of <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">carbon‑12</a> atoms. Carbon‑12 (<sup>12</sup>C) is an <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of carbon. The <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mole</a> is currently defined as "the quantity of entities (elementary particles like atoms or molecules) equal to the number of atoms in 12 grams of carbon‑12". Thus, the current definition of the mole requires that <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1000</sup>⁄<sub>12</sub></span> (83⅓) moles of <sup>12</sup>C has a mass of precisely one kilogram. The number of atoms in a mole, a quantity known as the <a href="/wiki/Avogadro_constant" title="Avogadro constant">Avogadro constant</a>, is experimentally determined, and the current best estimate of its value is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7023602214085700000♠</span>6.022<span style="margin-left:.25em;">140</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">857</span>(74)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>23</sup></span> entities per mole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physconst-NA_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-physconst-NA-89">[72]</a></sup> This new definition of the kilogram proposed to fix the Avogadro constant at precisely <span style="white-space:nowrap">6.022<span style="margin-left:0.25em">14X</span><span style="margin-left:0.27em;margin-right:0.27em">×</span>10<span style="display:none">^</span><sup>23</sup></span> with the kilogram being defined as "the mass equal to that of <span class="nowrap"><span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1000</sup>⁄<sub>12</sub></span> · <span style="white-space:nowrap">6.022<span style="margin-left:0.25em">14X</span><span style="margin-left:0.27em;margin-right:0.27em">×</span>10<span style="display:none">^</span><sup>23</sup></span></span> atoms of <sup>12</sup>C".</p>
<p>The accuracy of the measured value of the Avogadro constant is currently limited by the uncertainty in the value of the <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">Planck constant</a>—a measure relating the energy of photons to their frequency. That relative standard uncertainty has been 50<span class="nowrap"> </span>parts per billion (ppb) since 2006. By fixing the Avogadro constant, the practical effect of this proposal would be that the uncertainty in the mass of a <sup>12</sup>C atom—and the magnitude of the kilogram—could be no better than the current 50<span class="nowrap"> </span>ppb uncertainty in the Planck constant. Under this proposal, the magnitude of the kilogram would be subject to future refinement as improved measurements of the value of the Planck constant become available; electronic realizations of the kilogram would be recalibrated as required. Conversely, an electronic <i>definition</i> of the kilogram (see <i><a href="#Electronic_approaches">Electronic approaches</a></i>, below), which would precisely fix the Planck constant, would continue to allow 83⅓ moles of <sup>12</sup>C to have a mass of precisely one kilogram but the number of atoms comprising a mole (the Avogadro constant) would continue to be subject to future refinement.</p>
<p>A variation on a <sup>12</sup>C-based definition proposes to define the Avogadro constant as being precisely <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7007844468890000000♠</span>84<span style="margin-left:.25em;">446</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">889</span></span><sup>3</sup> (≈ <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7023602214162000000♠</span>6.022<span style="margin-left:.25em;">141</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">62</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>23</sup></span>) atoms. An imaginary realization of a 12-gram mass prototype would be a cube of <sup>12</sup>C atoms measuring precisely <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7007844468890000000♠</span>84<span style="margin-left:.25em;">446</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">889</span></span> atoms across on a side. With this proposal, the kilogram would be defined as "the mass equal to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7010844468890000000♠</span>84<span style="margin-left:.25em;">446</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">889</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>3</sup></span><span class="nowrap"> </span>× 83⅓ atoms of <sup>12</sup>C."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[Note 18]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Avogadro_project">Avogadro project</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Avogadro project">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><span id="Avogadro_project"></span></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="863" data-file-width="631" height="301" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg/220px-Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg/330px-Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg/440px-Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Achim_Leistner" title="Achim Leistner">Achim Leistner</a> at the <a class="external text" href="http://www.acpo.csiro.au/" rel="nofollow">Australian Centre for Precision Optics</a> (ACPO) is holding a 1<span class="nowrap"> </span>kg, single-crystal silicon sphere for the Avogadro project. These spheres are among the roundest man-made objects in the world. If the best of these spheres were scaled to the size of Earth, its high point—a continent-size area—would rise to a maximum elevation of 2.4 metres above "sea level".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[Note 19]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another Avogadro constant-based approach, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Avogadro_Coordination" title="International Avogadro Coordination">International Avogadro Coordination</a>'s <i>Avogadro project</i>, would define and delineate the kilogram as a 93.6<span class="nowrap"> </span>mm diameter sphere of <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> atoms. Silicon was chosen because a commercial infrastructure with <a href="/wiki/Czochralski_process" title="Czochralski process">mature processes</a> for creating defect-free, ultra-pure monocrystalline silicon already exists to service the <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> industry. To make a practical realization of the kilogram, a silicon <a href="/wiki/Boule_(crystal)" title="Boule (crystal)">boule</a> (a rod-like, single-crystal ingot) would be produced. Its <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopic</a> composition would be measured with a <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometer</a> to determine its average relative atomic mass. The boule would be cut, ground, and polished into spheres. The size of a select sphere would be measured using optical <a href="/wiki/Interferometry" title="Interferometry">interferometry</a> to an uncertainty of about 0.3<span class="nowrap"> </span>nm on the radius—roughly a single atomic layer. The precise lattice spacing between the atoms in its crystal structure (≈192<span class="nowrap"> </span>pm) would be measured using a scanning X-ray interferometer. This permits its atomic spacing to be determined with an uncertainty of only three parts per billion. With the size of the sphere, its average atomic mass, and its atomic spacing known, the required sphere diameter can be calculated with sufficient precision and low uncertainty to enable it to be finish-polished to a target mass of one kilogram.</p>
<p>Experiments are being performed on the Avogadro Project's silicon spheres to determine whether their masses are most stable when stored in a vacuum, a partial vacuum, or ambient pressure. However, no technical means currently exist to prove a long-term stability any better than that of the IPK's because the most sensitive and accurate measurements of mass are made with <a href="/wiki/Mass_versus_weight#Types_of_scales_and_what_they_measure" title="Mass versus weight">dual-pan</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beam_balance" title="Beam balance">balances</a> like the BIPM's FB‑2 flexure-strip balance (see <i><a href="#External_links">External links</a></i>, below). Balances can only compare the mass of a silicon sphere to that of a reference mass. Given the latest understanding of the lack of long-term mass stability with the IPK and its replicas, there is no known, perfectly stable mass artifact to compare against. <a href="/wiki/Mass_versus_weight#Types_of_scales_and_what_they_measure" title="Mass versus weight">Single-pan</a> <a href="/wiki/Weighing_scale" title="Weighing scale">scales</a>, which measure weight relative to an invariant of nature, are not precise to the necessary long-term uncertainty of <span class="nowrap">10–20</span> parts per billion. Another issue to be overcome is that silicon oxidizes and forms a thin layer (equivalent to <span class="nowrap">5–20</span> silicon atoms) of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a> (<a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Silicon_monoxide" title="Silicon monoxide">silicon monoxide</a>. This layer slightly increases the mass of the sphere, an effect that must be accounted for when polishing the sphere to its finished dimension. Oxidation is not an issue with platinum and iridium, both of which are <a href="/wiki/Noble_metal" title="Noble metal">noble metals</a> that are roughly as <a href="/wiki/Galvanic_series" title="Galvanic series">cathodic</a> as oxygen and therefore don't oxidize unless coaxed to do so in the laboratory. The presence of the thin oxide layer on a silicon-sphere mass prototype places additional restrictions on the procedures that might be suitable to clean it to avoid changing the layer's thickness or oxide <a href="/wiki/Stoichiometry" title="Stoichiometry">stoichiometry</a>.</p>
<p>All silicon-based approaches would fix the Avogadro constant but vary in the details of the definition of the kilogram. One approach would use silicon with all three of its natural isotopes present. About 7.78% of silicon comprises the two heavier isotopes: <sup>29</sup>Si and <sup>30</sup>Si. As described in <i><a href="#Carbon-12">Carbon‑12</a></i> above, this method would <i>define</i> the magnitude of the kilogram in terms of a certain number of <sup>12</sup>C atoms by fixing the Avogadro constant; the silicon sphere would be the <i>practical realization</i>. This approach could accurately delineate the magnitude of the kilogram because the masses of the three silicon <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclides</a> relative to <sup>12</sup>C are known with great precision (relative uncertainties of 1<span class="nowrap"> </span><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" title="Parts per billion">ppb</a> or better). An alternative method for creating a silicon sphere-based kilogram proposes to use <a href="/wiki/Isotope_separation" title="Isotope separation">isotopic separation</a> techniques to enrich the silicon until it is nearly pure <sup>28</sup>Si, which has a relative atomic mass of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001279769265325000♠</span>27.976<span style="margin-left:.25em;">926</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">5325</span>(19)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AvogadroNature_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-AvogadroNature-94">[75]</a></sup> With this approach, the Avogadro constant would not only be fixed, but so too would the atomic mass of <sup>28</sup>Si. As such, the definition of the kilogram would be decoupled from <sup>12</sup>C and the kilogram would instead be defined as <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1000</sup>⁄<sub><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001279769265325000♠</span>27.976<span style="margin-left:.25em;">926</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">5325</span></span></sub></span><span class="nowrap"> </span><b>·</b><span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7023602214179000000♠</span>6.022<span style="margin-left:.25em;">141</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">79</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>23</sup></span> atoms of <sup>28</sup>Si (≈<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001357437404300000♠</span>35.743<span style="margin-left:.25em;">740</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">43</span></span> fixed moles of <sup>28</sup>Si atoms). Physicists could elect to define the kilogram in terms of <sup>28</sup>Si even when kilogram prototypes are made of natural silicon (all three isotopes present). Even with a kilogram definition based on theoretically pure <sup>28</sup>Si, a silicon-sphere prototype made of only nearly pure <sup>28</sup>Si would necessarily deviate slightly from the defined number of moles of silicon to compensate for various chemical and isotopic impurities as well as the effect of surface oxides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ion_accumulation">Ion accumulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ion accumulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Another Avogadro-based approach, <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ion</a> accumulation, since abandoned, would have defined and delineated the kilogram by precisely creating new metal prototypes on demand. It would have done so by accumulating <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a> <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> (atoms stripped of an electron) and counted them by measuring the electric current required to neutralize the ions. Gold (<sup>197</sup>Au) and bismuth (<sup>209</sup>Bi) were chosen because they can be safely handled and have the two highest <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic masses</a> among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mononuclidic_elements" title="Mononuclidic elements">mononuclidic elements</a> that is effectively non-radioactive (bismuth) or is perfectly stable (gold). See also <i><a href="/wiki/Table_of_nuclides_(combined)" title="Table of nuclides (combined)">Table of nuclides</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[Note 20]</a></sup></p>
<p>With a gold-based definition of the kilogram for instance, the relative atomic mass of gold could have been fixed as precisely <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002196966568700000♠</span>196.966<span style="margin-left:.25em;">5687</span></span>, from the current value of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002196966568700000♠</span>196.966<span style="margin-left:.25em;">5687</span>(6)</span>. As with a definition based upon carbon‑12, the Avogadro constant would also have been fixed. The kilogram would then have been defined as "the mass equal to that of precisely <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1000</sup>⁄<sub><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002196966568700000♠</span>196.966<span style="margin-left:.25em;">5687</span></span></sub></span><span class="nowrap"> </span><b>·</b><span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7023602214179000000♠</span>6.022<span style="margin-left:.25em;">141</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">79</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>23</sup></span> atoms of gold" (precisely 3,057,443,620,887,933,963,384,315 atoms of gold or about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000507700370999999♠</span>5.077<span style="margin-left:.25em;">003</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">71</span></span> fixed moles).</p>
<p>In 2003, German experiments with gold at a current of only 10<span class="nowrap"> </span>µA demonstrated a relative uncertainty of 1.5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PTB_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-PTB-97">[77]</a></sup> Follow-on experiments using bismuth ions and a current of 30<span class="nowrap"> </span>mA were expected to accumulate a mass of 30<span class="nowrap"> </span>g in six days and to have a relative uncertainty of better than 1 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[78]</a></sup> Ultimately, ion‑accumulation approaches proved to be unsuitable. Measurements required months and the data proved too erratic for the technique to be considered a viable future replacement to the IPK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among the many technical challenges of the ion-deposition apparatus was obtaining a sufficiently high ion current (mass deposition rate) while simultaneously decelerating the ions so they could all deposit onto a target electrode embedded in a balance pan. Experiments with gold showed the ions had to be decelerated to very low energies to avoid <a href="/wiki/Sputtering" title="Sputtering">sputtering</a> effects—a phenomenon whereby ions that had already been counted ricochet off the target electrode or even dislodged atoms that had already been deposited. The deposited mass fraction in the 2003 German experiments only approached very close to 100% at ion energies of less than around 1<span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a> (&lt;1<span class="nowrap"> </span>km/s for gold).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PTB_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-PTB-97">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>If the kilogram had been defined as a precise quantity of gold or bismuth atoms deposited with an electric current, not only would the Avogadro constant and the atomic mass of gold or bismuth have to have been precisely fixed, but also the value of the <a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge">elementary charge</a> (<i>e</i>), likely to <span style="white-space:nowrap">1.602<span style="margin-left:0.25em">17X</span><span style="margin-left:0.27em;margin-right:0.27em">×</span>10<span style="display:none">^</span><sup>−19</sup> <a href="/wiki/Coulomb" title="Coulomb">C</a></span> (from the currently recommended value of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6981160217662079999♠</span>1.602<span style="margin-left:.25em;">176</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">6208</span>(98)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−19</sup> C</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physconst-e_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-physconst-e-100">[80]</a></sup>). Doing so would have effectively defined the <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">ampere</a> as a flow of <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub><span style="white-space:nowrap">1.602<span style="margin-left:0.25em">17X</span><span style="margin-left:0.27em;margin-right:0.27em">×</span>10<span style="display:none">^</span><sup>−19</sup></span></sub></span> electrons per second past a fixed point in an electric circuit. The SI unit of mass would have been fully defined by having precisely fixed the values of the Avogadro constant and elementary charge, and by exploiting the fact that the atomic masses of bismuth and gold atoms are invariant, universal constants of nature.</p>
<p>Beyond the slowness of making a new mass standard and the poor reproducibility, there were other intrinsic shortcomings to the ion‑accumulation approach that proved to be formidable obstacles to ion-accumulation-based techniques becoming a practical realization. The apparatus necessarily required that the deposition chamber have an integral balance system to enable the convenient calibration of a reasonable quantity of transfer standards relative to any single internal ion-deposited prototype. Furthermore, the mass prototypes produced by ion deposition techniques would have been nothing like the freestanding platinum-iridium prototypes currently in use; they would have been deposited onto—and become part of—an electrode imbedded into one pan of a special balance integrated into the device. Moreover, the ion-deposited mass wouldn't have had a hard, highly polished surface that can be vigorously cleaned like those of current prototypes. Gold, while dense and a <a href="/wiki/Noble_metal" title="Noble metal">noble metal</a> (resistant to oxidation and the formation of other compounds), is extremely soft so an internal gold prototype would have to be kept well isolated and scrupulously clean to avoid contamination and the potential of wear from having to remove the contamination. Bismuth, which is an inexpensive metal used in low-temperature solders, slowly oxidizes when exposed to room-temperature air and forms other chemical compounds and so would not have produced stable reference masses unless it was continually maintained in a vacuum or inert atmosphere.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ampere-based_force">Ampere-based force</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ampere-based force">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1716" data-file-width="2400" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg/220px-Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg/330px-Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg/440px-Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meissner_effect_p1390048.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A magnet floating above a superconductor bathed in liquid nitrogen demonstrates <a href="/wiki/Superdiamagnetism" title="Superdiamagnetism">perfect diamagnetic</a> levitation via the <a href="/wiki/Meissner_effect" title="Meissner effect">Meissner effect</a>. Experiments with an ampere-based definition of the kilogram flipped this arrangement upside-down: an electric field accelerated a superconducting test mass supported by fixed magnets.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>This approach would define the kilogram as "the mass which would be accelerated at precisely <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6993200000000000000♠</span>2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−7</sup> m/s<sup>2</sup></span> when subjected to the per-metre force between two straight parallel conductors of infinite length, of negligible circular cross section, placed one metre apart in vacuum, through which flow a constant current of <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub><span style="white-space:nowrap">1.602<span style="margin-left:0.25em">17X</span><span style="margin-left:0.27em;margin-right:0.27em">×</span>10<span style="display:none">^</span><sup>−19</sup></span></sub></span> elementary charges per second".</p>
<p>Effectively, this would define the kilogram as a derivative of the <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">ampere</a> rather than the present relationship, which defines the ampere as a derivative of the kilogram. This redefinition of the kilogram would specify <a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge">elementary charge</a> (<i>e</i>) as precisely <span style="white-space:nowrap">1.602<span style="margin-left:0.25em">17X</span><span style="margin-left:0.27em;margin-right:0.27em">×</span>10<span style="display:none">^</span><sup>−19</sup></span> <a href="/wiki/Coulomb" title="Coulomb">coulomb</a> rather than the current recommended value of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6981160217662079999♠</span>1.602<span style="margin-left:.25em;">176</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">6208</span>(98)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−19</sup> C</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physconst-e_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-physconst-e-100">[80]</a></sup> It would necessarily follow that the ampere (one coulomb per second) would also become an electric current of this precise quantity of elementary charges per second passing a given point in an electric circuit. The virtue of a practical realization based upon this definition is that unlike the Kibble balance and other scale-based methods, all of which require the careful characterization of gravity in the laboratory, this method delineates the magnitude of the kilogram directly in the very terms that define the nature of mass: acceleration due to an applied force. Unfortunately, it is extremely difficult to develop a practical realization based upon accelerating masses. Experiments over a period of years in Japan with a <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconducting</a>, 30<span class="nowrap"> </span>g mass supported by <a href="/wiki/Diamagnetism" title="Diamagnetism">diamagnetic</a> levitation never achieved an uncertainty better than ten parts per million. <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_hysteresis" title="Magnetic hysteresis">Magnetic hysteresis</a> was one of the limiting issues. Other groups performed similar research that used different techniques to levitate the mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="SI_multiples">SI multiples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: SI multiples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(mass)" title="Orders of magnitude (mass)">Orders of magnitude (mass)</a></div>
<p>Because <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI_prefix" title="SI prefix">SI prefixes</a> may not be concatenated (serially linked) within the name or symbol for a unit of measure, SI prefixes are used with the <i><a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">gram</a></i>, not the kilogram, which already has a prefix as part of its name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[83]</a></sup> For instance, one-millionth of a kilogram is 1<span class="nowrap"> </span>mg (one milligram), not 1<span class="nowrap"> </span>µkg (one microkilogram).</p>

</div>
<p><br/></p>
<ul>
<li>The microgram is typically abbreviated "mcg" in pharmaceutical and nutritional supplement labelling, to avoid confusion, since the "µ" prefix is not always well recognized outside of technical disciplines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[Note 22]</a></sup> (The expression "mcg" is also the symbol for an obsolete <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centimetre_gram_second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre gram second system of units">CGS</a> unit of measure known as the "millicentigram", which is equal to 10<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg.)</li>
<li>In the UK, because serious medication errors have been made from the confusion between milligrams and micrograms when micrograms has been abbreviated, the recommendation given in the Scottish Palliative Care Guidelines is that doses of less than one milligram <i>must</i> be expressed in micrograms and that the word microgram <i>must</i> be written in full, and that it is never acceptable to use "mcg" or "μg".<a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.palliativecareguidelines.scot.nhs.uk/guidelines/about-the-guidelines/pharmacological-considerations/prescribing-information-for-liquid-medicines.aspx" rel="nofollow">[2]</a></li>
<li>The decagram (dag in SI) is in much of Europe often abbreviated "dkg" (from the local spelling "dekagram") and is used for typical retail quantities of food (such as cheese and meat).</li>
<li>The unit name "megagram" is rarely used, and even then typically only in technical fields in contexts where especially rigorous consistency with the SI standard is desired. For most purposes, the name "<a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonne</a>" is instead used. The tonne and its symbol, "t", were adopted by the CIPM in 1879. It is a non-SI unit accepted by the BIPM for use with the SI. According to the BIPM, "In English speaking countries this unit is usually called 'metric ton'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[84]</a></sup> The unit name "megatonne" or "megaton" (Mt) is often used in general-interest literature on <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions, whereas the equivalent unit in scientific papers on the subject is often the "teragram" (Tg).</li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kilogram&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The avoirdupois pound is part of both <a href="/wiki/United_States_customary_units" title="United States customary units">United States customary system of units</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_units" title="Imperial units">Imperial system of units</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The spelling <i>kilogram</i> is the modern spelling used by the International Bureau of Weights and Measures (BIPM), the US <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">National Institute of Standards and Technology</a> (NIST), the UK's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Measurement_Office" title="National Measurement Office">National Measurement Office</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Research_Council_of_Canada" title="National Research Council of Canada">National Research Council of Canada</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/National_Measurement_Institute,_Australia" title="National Measurement Institute, Australia">National Measurement Institute, Australia</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The French text (which is the authoritative text) states "<i>Il n'est pas autorisé d'utiliser des abréviations pour les symboles et noms d'unités ...</i>"</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In professional <a href="/wiki/Metrology" title="Metrology">metrology</a> (the science of measurement), the acceleration of Earth's gravity is taken as <a href="/wiki/Standard_gravity" title="Standard gravity">standard gravity</a> (symbol: <i>g</i><sub>n</sub>), which is defined as precisely <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000980665000000000♠</span>9.806<span style="margin-left:.25em;">65</span></span><span class="nowrap"> </span><a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second_squared" title="Metre per second squared">metres per square second</a> (m/s<sup>2</sup>). The expression <span class="nowrap">"1 m/s<sup>2</sup>"</span> means that <i>for every second that elapses</i>, velocity changes an additional 1 metre per second. In more familiar terms: an acceleration of 1<span class="nowrap"> </span>m/s<sup>2</sup> can also be expressed as a rate of change in velocity of precisely 3.6 km/h per second (≈2.2 mph per second).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Matter has invariant mass assuming it is not traveling at a <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_special_relativity" title="Mass in special relativity">relativistic</a> speed with respect to an observer. According to <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein's</a> theory of <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_special_relativity" title="Mass in special relativity">relativistic mass</a> (apparent mass with respect to an observer) of an object or particle with rest mass <i>m</i><sub>0</sub> increases with its speed as <span class="nowrap"><i>M</i> =<i>γm</i><sub>0</sub></span> (where <i>γ</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_factor" title="Lorentz factor">Lorentz factor</a>). This effect is vanishingly small at everyday speeds, which are many <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">orders of magnitude</a> less than the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. For example, to change the mass of a kilogram by 1 μg (1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" title="Parts per billion">ppb</a>, about the level of detection by current technology) would require moving it at 0.0045% of the speed of light relative to an observer, which is 13.4 km/s (30,000 mph). As regards the kilogram, relativity's effect upon the constancy of matter's mass is simply an interesting scientific phenomenon that has zero effect on the definition of the kilogram and its practical realizations.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The same decree also defined the litre as follows: "Liter: the measure of volume, both for liquid and solids, for which the displacement would be that of a cube [with sides measuring] one-tenth of a metre." Original text: "<span lang="fr"><i>Litre</i>, la mesure de capacité, tant pour les liquides que pour les matières sèches, dont la contenance sera celle du cube de la dixièrne partie du mètre.</span>"</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Modern measurements show the temperature at which water reaches maximum density is 3.984<span class="nowrap"> </span>°C. However, the scientists at the close of the 18th century concluded that the temperature was 4<span class="nowrap"> </span>°C.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The provisional kilogram standard had been fabricated in accordance with a single, inaccurate measurement of the density of water made earlier by <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Just_Ha%C3%BCy" title="René Just Haüy">René Just Haüy</a>, which showed that one cubic decimetre of distilled water at 0<span class="nowrap"> </span>°C had a mass of 18,841 grains in France's soon-to-be-obsoleted <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_units_of_measurement" title="French units of measurement">poids de marc</a></i> system. The newer, highly accurate measurements by Lefèvre‑Gineau and Fabbroni concluded that the mass of a cubic decimetre of water at the new temperature of 4<span class="nowrap"> </span>°C—a condition at which water is denser—was actually <i>less massive</i>, at 18,827.15 grains, than the earlier inaccurate value assumed for 0<span class="nowrap"> </span>°C water. France's metric system had been championed by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Maurice_de_Talleyrand-P%C3%A9rigord" title="Charles Maurice de Talleyrand-Périgord">Charles Maurice de Talleyrand‑Périgord</a>. On March 30, 1791, four days after Talleyrand forwarded a specific proposal on how to proceed with the project, the French government ordered a committee known as the Academy to commence work on accurately determining the magnitude of the base units of the new metric system. The Academy divided the task among five commissions. The commission charged with determining the mass of a cubic decimetre of water originally comprised Lavoisier and Haüy but their work was finished by Louis Lefèvre‑Gineau and Giovanni Fabbroni. Neither Lavoisier nor Haüy can be blamed for participating in an initial—and inaccurate—measurement and for leaving the final work to Lefèvre‑Gineau and Fabbroni to finish in 1799. As a member of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ferme_g%C3%A9n%C3%A9rale" title="Ferme générale">Ferme générale</a></i>, Lavoisier was also one of France's 28 tax collectors. He was consequently convicted of treason during the waning days of the <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Terror" title="Reign of Terror">Reign of Terror</a> period of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> and beheaded on May 8, 1794. Lavoisier's partner, Haüy, was also thrown into prison and was himself at risk of going to the guillotine but his life was spared after a renowned French naturalist interceded.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Pavillon's (and hence the BIPM's) postal address is in the neighboring commune of <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A8vres" title="Sèvres">Sèvres</a>, so it is often reported as being located there, but the grounds are on the commune of Saint-Cloud (<a class="external text" href="https://www.openstreetmap.org/#map=17/48.82897/2.21983" rel="nofollow">OpenStreetMap</a>).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-n841-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-n841_46-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-n841_46-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Prototype No. 8(41) was accidentally stamped with the number 41, but its accessories carry the proper number 8. Since there is no prototype marked 8, this prototype is referred to as 8(41).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-n650-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-n650_48-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Nos. 42′, 77 and 650 are called "standards" rather than "prototypes" because they are slightly underweight, slightly too much material having been removed when they were manufactured. Other than being more than 1 mg below the nominal 1 kg mass, they are identical to the prototypes, and are used during routine calibration work.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The other two Pt‑10Ir standards owned by the US are K79, from a new series of prototypes <span class="nowrap">(K64–K80)</span> that were diamond-turned directly to a finish mass, and K85, which is used for Kibble balance experiments (see <i><a href="#Kibble_balance">Kibble balance</a></i>, above).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Note that if the 50 µg difference between the IPK and its replicas was entirely due to wear, the IPK would have to have lost 150 million billion more platinum and iridium atoms over the last century than its replicas. That there would be this much wear, much less a <i>difference</i> of this magnitude, is thought unlikely; 50 µg is roughly the mass of a fingerprint. Specialists at the BIPM in 1946 carefully conducted cleaning experiments and concluded that even <i>vigorous</i> rubbing with a chamois—if done carefully—did not alter the prototypes' mass. More recent cleaning experiments at the BIPM, which were conducted on one particular prototype (K63), and which benefited from the then-new NBS‑2 balance, demonstrated 2 µg stability. Experiments on prototypes No. 7 and 32 in January 2014 showed less than 0.5 µg mass lost from a third complete cleaning and washing cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stock_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stock-49">[38]</a></sup><br/>
    Many theories have been advanced to explain the divergence in the masses of the prototypes. One theory posits that the relative change in mass between the IPK and its replicas is not one of loss at all and is instead a simple matter that the IPK has <i>gained less</i> than the replicas. This theory begins with the observation that the IPK is uniquely stored under three nested bell jars whereas its six sister copies stored alongside it in the vault as well as the other replicas dispersed throughout the world are stored under only two. This theory is also founded on two other facts: that platinum has a strong affinity for mercury, and that atmospheric mercury is significantly more abundant in the atmosphere today than at the time the IPK and its replicas were manufactured. The burning of coal is a major contributor to atmospheric mercury and both Denmark and Germany have high coal shares in electrical generation. Conversely, electrical generation in France, where the IPK is stored, is mostly nuclear. This theory is supported by the fact that the mass divergence rate—relative to the IPK—of Denmark's prototype, K48, since it took possession in 1949 is an especially high 78 µg per century while that of Germany's prototype has been even greater at <span class="nowrap">126 µg/century</span> ever since it took possession of K55 in 1954. However, still other data for other replicas isn't supportive of this theory. This mercury absorption theory is just one of many advanced by the specialists to account for the relative change in mass. To date, each theory has either proven implausible, or there are insufficient data or technical means to either prove or disprove it.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The mean change in mass of the first batch of replicas relative to the IPK over one hundred years is +23.5<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg with a standard deviation of 30<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg. Per <i>The Third Periodic Verification of National Prototypes of the Kilogram <span class="nowrap">(1988–1992)</span></i>, G.<span class="nowrap"> </span>Girard, Metrologia <b>31</b> (1994) Pg.<span class="nowrap"> </span>323, Table<span class="nowrap"> </span>3. Data is for prototypes K1, K5, K6, K7, K8(41), K12, K16, K18, K20, K21, K24, K32, K34, K35, K36, K37, K38, and K40; and excludes K2, K23, and K39, which are treated as outliers. This is a larger data set than is shown in the chart at the top of this section, which corresponds to Figure<span class="nowrap"> </span>7 of G.<span class="nowrap"> </span>Girard's paper.<sub><span class="nowrap"> </span></sub></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Assuming the past trend continues, whereby the mean change in mass of the first batch of replicas relative to the IPK over one hundred years was +23.5<span class="nowrap"> </span>σ<span style="margin-left:0.3em">30<span class="nowrap"> </span>µg.</span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The combined relative standard uncertainty (CRSU) of these measurements, as with all other tolerances and uncertainties in this article unless otherwise noted, have a 1σ standard deviation, which equates to a confidence level of about 68%; that is to say, 68% of the measurements fall within the stated tolerance.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Planck constant's unit of measure, the joule-second (J·s), may perhaps be more easily understood when expressed as a <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joule</a> per <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">hertz</a> (J/Hz). Universally, an individual photon has an energy that is proportional to its frequency. This relationship is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6966662607004000000♠</span>6.626<span style="margin-left:.25em;">070</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">040</span>(81)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−34</sup></span> J/Hz.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-92">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The proposal originally was to redefine the kilogram as the mass of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7007844468860000000♠</span>84<span style="margin-left:.25em;">446</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">886</span></span><sup>3</sup> carbon-12 atoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[74]</a></sup> The value <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7007844468860000000♠</span>84<span style="margin-left:.25em;">446</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">886</span></span> had been chosen because it has a special property; its cube (the proposed new value for the Avogadro constant) is divisible by twelve. Thus with that definition of the kilogram, there would have been an integer number of atoms in one gram of <sup>12</sup>C: <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7022501845081902290♠</span>50<span style="margin-left:.25em;">184</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">508</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">190</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">229</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">061</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">679</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">538</span></span> atoms. The uncertainty in the Avogadro constant has narrowed considerably since this proposal was first submitted to <i><a href="/wiki/American_Scientist" title="American Scientist">American Scientist</a></i> for publication. The 2014 CODATA value for the Avogadro constant (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7023602214085700000♠</span>6.022<span style="margin-left:.25em;">140</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">857</span>(74)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>23</sup></span>) has a relative standard uncertainty of 12 parts per billion and the cube root of this number is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7007844468854100000♠</span>84<span style="margin-left:.25em;">446</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">885</span>.41(35)</span>, i.e. there are no integers within the range of uncertainty.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-93">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The sphere shown in the photograph has an out-of-roundness value (peak to valley on the radius) of 50<span class="nowrap"> </span>nm. According to ACPO, they improved on that with an out-of-roundness of 35<span class="nowrap"> </span>nm. On the 93.6<span class="nowrap"> </span>mm diameter sphere, an out-of-roundness of 35<span class="nowrap"> </span>nm (undulations of ±17.5<span class="nowrap"> </span>nm) is a fractional roundness <span class="nowrap">(∆<i>r</i> /<i>r</i> ) = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6993370000000000000♠</span>3.7<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−7</sup></span>.</span> Scaled to the size of Earth, this is equivalent to a maximum deviation from sea level of only 2.4<span class="nowrap"> </span>m. The roundness of that ACPO sphere is exceeded only by two of the four <a href="/wiki/Fused_quartz" title="Fused quartz">fused-quartz</a> gyroscope rotors flown on <a href="/wiki/Gravity_Probe_B" title="Gravity Probe B">Gravity Probe<span class="nowrap"> </span>B</a>, which were manufactured in the late 1990s and given their final figure at the <a class="external text" href="http://hepl.stanford.edu/" rel="nofollow">W.W. Hansen Experimental Physics Lab</a> at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>. Particularly, "Gyro 4" is recorded in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinness_world_records" title="Guinness world records">Guinness</a> database of world records (their database, not in their book) as <i>the</i> world's roundest man-made object. According to a published report (<a class="external text" href="http://aa.stanford.edu/aeroastro/posters2007/Polhode_Motion.pdf" rel="nofollow">221<span class="nowrap"> </span>kB PDF, here</a><span style="margin-left:0.2em">)</span> and the GP‑B public affairs coordinator at Stanford University, of the four gyroscopes onboard the probe, Gyro<span class="nowrap"> </span>4 has a maximum surface undulation from a perfect sphere of 3.4<span class="nowrap"> </span>±0.4<span class="nowrap"> </span>nm on the 38.1<span class="nowrap"> </span>mm diameter sphere, which is a <span class="nowrap">∆<i>r</i> /<i>r</i> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6993180000000000000♠</span>1.8<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−7</sup></span>.</span> Scaled to the size of Earth, this is equivalent to an undulation the size of North America rising slowly up out of the sea (in molecular-layer terraces 11.9<span class="nowrap"> </span>cm high), reaching a maximum elevation of 1.14<span class="nowrap"> </span>±0.13<span class="nowrap"> </span>m in Nebraska, and then gradually sloping back down to sea level on the other side of the continent.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-96">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 2003, the same year the first gold-deposition experiments were conducted, physicists found that the only naturally occurring isotope of bismuth, <sup>209</sup>Bi, is actually very slightly <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a>, with the longest known radioactive <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of any naturally occurring element that decays via <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha radiation</a>—a half-life of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7026599594400000000♠</span>(19<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>2)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>18</sup> years</span>. As this is 1.4 billion times the age of the universe, <sup>209</sup>Bi is considered a stable isotope for most practical applications (those unrelated to such disciplines as <a href="/wiki/Nucleocosmochronology" title="Nucleocosmochronology">nucleocosmochronology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geochronology" title="Geochronology">geochronology</a>). In other terms, <span class="nowrap"><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001999999999830000♠</span>99.999<span style="margin-left:.25em;">999</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">983</span></span>%</span> of the bismuth that existed on Earth 4.567 billion years ago still exists today. Only two mononuclidic elements are heavier than bismuth and only one approaches its stability: <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>. Long considered a possible replacement for uranium in nuclear reactors, thorium can cause cancer when inhaled because it is over 1.2<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion times more radioactive than bismuth. It also has such a strong tendency to oxidize that its powders are <a href="/wiki/Pyrophoricity" title="Pyrophoricity">pyrophoric</a>. These characteristics make thorium unsuitable in ion-deposition experiments. See also <i><a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_bismuth" title="Isotopes of bismuth">Isotopes of bismuth</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_gold" title="Isotopes of gold">Isotopes of gold</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_thorium" title="Isotopes of thorium">Isotopes of thorium</a></i>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-104"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-104">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Criterion: A combined total of at least five occurrences on the <a href="/wiki/British_National_Corpus" title="British National Corpus">British National Corpus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Corpus_of_Contemporary_American_English" title="Corpus of Contemporary American English">Corpus of Contemporary American English</a>, including both the singular and the plural for both the -<i>gram</i> and the -<i>gramme</i> spelling.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The practice of using the abbreviation "mcg" rather than the SI symbol "µg" was formally mandated in the US for medical practitioners in 2004 by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joint_Commission_on_Accreditation_of_Healthcare_Organizations" title="Joint Commission on Accreditation of Healthcare Organizations">Joint Commission on Accreditation of Healthcare Organizations</a> (JCAHO) in their <a class="external text" href="https://www.aapmr.org/practice/guidelines/Pages/New-Joint-Commission-symbols.aspx" rel="nofollow">"Do Not Use" List: Abbreviations, Acronyms, and Symbols</a> because "µg" and "mg" when handwritten can be confused with one another, resulting in a thousand-fold overdosing (or underdosing). The mandate was also adopted by the <a class="external text" href="http://www.ismp.org/" rel="nofollow">Institute for Safe Medication Practices</a>.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Gramme, le poids absolu d'un volume d'eau pure égal au cube de la centième partie du mètre, et à la température de la glace fondante.</i>; The term <i>poids absolu</i> was at the time used alongside <i>masse</i> for the concept of "mass" (which latter term had first been introduced in its strict physical sense in English in 1704). See e.g. Mathurin Jacques Brisson, <i>Dictionnaire raisonné de toutes les parties de la Physique</i>, Volland, 1787, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=1i0mJteVrOoC&amp;pg=PA401" rel="nofollow">p. 401</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-SIbrochure8th-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-SIbrochure8th_8-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-SIbrochure8th_8-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFInternational_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures2006"><a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a> (2006), <a class="external text" href="http://www.bipm.org/utils/common/pdf/si_brochure_8_en.pdf" rel="nofollow"><i>The International System of Units (SI)</i></a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span> (8th ed.), <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/92-822-2213-6" title="Special:BookSources/92-822-2213-6">92-822-2213-6</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20170814094625/http://www.bipm.org/utils/common/pdf/si_brochure_8_en.pdf" rel="nofollow">archived</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span> from the original on 2017-08-14</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+International+System+of+Units+%28SI%29&amp;rft.edition=8th&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=92-822-2213-6&amp;rft.au=International+Bureau+of+Weights+and+Measures&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.bipm.org%2Futils%2Fcommon%2Fpdf%2Fsi_brochure_8_en.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKilogram"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-OED-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-OED_10-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-OED_10-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.oed.com/viewdictionaryentry:showfullentry/true?t:ac=Entry/103396" rel="nofollow">"Kilogram"</a>. <i>Oxford English Dictionary</i>. Oxford University Press<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">November 3,</span> 2011</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Oxford+English+Dictionary&amp;rft.atitle=Kilogram&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.oed.com%2Fviewdictionaryentry%3Ashowfullentry%2Ftrue%3Ft%3Aac%3DEntry%2F103396&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKilogram"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Fowlers, HW; Fowler, FG (1964). <i>The Concise Oxford Dictionary</i>. Oxford: The Clarendon Press.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Concise+Oxford+Dictionary&amp;rft.place=Oxford&amp;rft.pub=The+Clarendon+Press&amp;rft.date=1964&amp;rft.aulast=Fowlers&amp;rft.aufirst=HW&amp;rft.au=Fowler%2C+FG&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKilogram"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Greek <span lang="grc">γράμμα</span> (as it were <span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B3%CF%81%CE%AC%CF%86%CF%89" title="wikt:γράφω">γράφ</a>-<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-%CE%BC%CE%B1" title="wikt:-μα">μα</a></span>, Doric <span lang="grc">γράθμα</span>) means "something written, a letter", but it came to be used as a unit of weight, apparently equal to 1/24 of an <a href="/wiki/Ounce" title="Ounce">ounce</a> (1/288 of a <i><a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)#Roman_libra" title="Pound (mass)">libra</a></i>, which would correspond to about 1.14 grams in modern units), at some time during Late Antiquity. French <i>gramme</i> was adopted from Latin <i>gramma</i>, itself quite obscure, but found in the <i>carmen de ponderibus et mensuris</i> (8.25) attributed by <a href="/wiki/Remmius_Palaemon" title="Remmius Palaemon">Remmius Palaemon</a> (fl. 1st century), where it is the weight of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obolus" title="Obolus">oboli</a> (Charlton T. Lewis, Charles Short, <i>A Latin Dictionary</i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A1999.04.0059%3Aentry%3Dgramma2" rel="nofollow">s.v. "gramma"</a>, 1879). Henry George Liddell. Robert Scott. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Greek-English_Lexicon" title="A Greek-English Lexicon">A Greek-English Lexicon</a></i> (revised and augmented edition, Oxford, 1940) <a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus:text:1999.04.0057:entry=gra/mma" rel="nofollow">s.v. γράμμα</a>, citing the 10th-century work <i><a href="/wiki/Geoponica" title="Geoponica">Geoponica</a></i> and a 4th-century papyrus edited in L. Mitteis, <i>Griechische Urkunden der Papyrussammlung zu Leipzig</i>, vol. i (1906), 62 ii 27.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://mjp.univ-perp.fr/france/1793mesures.htm" rel="nofollow">"Décret relatif aux poids et aux mesures du 18 germinal an 3 (7 avril 1795)"</a> [Decree of 18 Germinal, year III (April 7, 1795) regarding weights and measures]. <i>Grandes lois de la République</i> (in French). Digithèque de matériaux juridiques et politiques, Université de Perpignan<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">November 3,</span> 2011</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Grandes+lois+de+la+R%C3%A9publique&amp;rft.atitle=D%C3%A9cret+relatif+aux+poids+et+aux+mesures+du+18+germinal+an+3+%287+avril+1795%29&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fmjp.univ-perp.fr%2Ffrance%2F1793mesures.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKilogram"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> <span class="error citation-comment" style="font-size:100%">Cite uses deprecated parameter <code style="color:inherit; border:inherit; padding:inherit;">|trans_title=</code> (<a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#deprecated_params" title="Help:CS1 errors">help</a>)</span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Convention nationale, décret du 1er août 1793, ed. Duvergier, <i>Collection complète des lois, décrets, ordonnances, règlemens avis du Conseil d'état, publiée sur les éditions officielles du Louvre</i>, vol. 6 (2nd ed. 1834), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=0mYZAAAAYAAJ&amp;pg=PA70#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">p. 70</a>. The <i>metre</i> (<i>mètre</i>) on which this definition depends was itself defined as the ten-millionth part of a quarter of Earth's <a href="/wiki/Meridian_(geography)" title="Meridian (geography)">meridian</a>, given in <a href="/wiki/Units_of_measurement_in_France_before_the_French_Revolution" title="Units of measurement in France before the French Revolution">traditional units</a> as 3 <i><a href="/wiki/Foot_(unit)" title="Foot (unit)">pieds</a></i>, 11.44 <i>lignes</i> (a <i>ligne</i> being the 12th part of an <i>pouce</i> (inch), or the 144th part of a <i>pied</i>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://english.oxforddictionaries.com/definition/kilogram" rel="nofollow">"Kilogram"</a>. <i>Oxford Dictionaries</i><span class="reference-accessdate">.
